server","slug":"web-server","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/214484"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=214484"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=214484"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=214484"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=214484"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2316419,"date":"2022-05-11T07:00:21","date_gmt":"2022-05-11T14:00:21","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2316419"},"modified":"2022-05-11T10:44:30","modified_gmt":"2022-05-11T17:44:30","slug":"socket-audit-open-source-code","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/05\/11\/socket-audit-open-source-code\/","title":{"rendered":"Socket lands $4.6M to audit and catch malicious open source code"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Securing the software supply chain is admittedly somewhat of a dry topic, but knowing which components and code go into your everyday devices and appliances is a critical part of the software development process that billions of people rely on every day.<\/p>\nSoftware is just like any other product you build and ship; it relies on using components that others have built, often in the form of source code, and making sure that it doesn’t break or have weaknesses that compromise the final product. Most of the world’s software relies on open source code that’s written by developers who publish their work for anyone to use. That also means a reliance on trusting that the developers will always act in good faith. But projects get abandoned and picked up by others who plant backdoors or malware, or, as seen recently since Russia’s invasion of Ukraine, a rise in “protestware,” in which open source software developers"https:\/\/www.technologyreview.com\/2022\/03\/21\/1047489\/activists-are-targeting-russians-with-open-source-protestware\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">alter their code<\/a> to wipe the contents of Russian computers in protest of the Kremlin’s incursion.<\/p>\nFeross Aboukhadijeh, a prolific open source maintainer and the founder of"https:\/\/socket.dev\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Socket<\/a>, told TechCrunch in a recent call that development teams often put too much trust in open source code, which can be catastrophic if a deliberate vulnerability is introduced into the supply chain and goes unnoticed.<\/p>\nSoftware is generally easier to fix than"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/08\/pony-ai-to-issue-recall-of-autonomous-driving-software\/\">autonomous cars<\/a> and"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/02\/fitbit-ionic-smartwatches-recalled-following-battery-burn-reports\/\">other hardware<\/a> that have to be recalled. But the consequences of a software compromise can be dire and widespread. Tainted software updates have led to"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/12\/17\/fireeye-breach-solarwinds-federal-agencies\/\">the mass compromise of U.S. federal government networks<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/05\/kaseya-hack-flood-ransomware\/\">ransomware attacks<\/a> and the targeting of enterprise password managers"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/08\/04\/passwordstate-supply-chain-attack\/\">aimed at stealing sensitive corporate secrets<\/a>.<\/p>\nAboukhadijeh"https:\/\/twitter.com\/feross\/status\/1498676284590800903\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">founded<\/a> Socket earlier this year alongside a team of fellow open source maintainers who have seen firsthand some of the worst software supply chain attacks in the wild. And so the team began work on building an app that developers can use to detect and block introducing potentially malicious code into their projects from millions of open source code repositories.<\/p>\nThe app plugs in to a GitHub developer’s account and runs through dozens of known behaviors, looking for package issues like potentially suspicious changes to the code, such as if an open source package you depend on suddenly starts trying to communicate over the network or getting shell access, which might indicate that the package has been compromised.<\/p>\nAboukhadijeh described Socket as offering a nutrition-fact label of an open source package’s capabilities by illuminating what access, permissions and behaviors a package has, like install scripts, which many kinds of malware use to hook into a victim’s system.<\/p>\n“We can’t tell you with certainty whether a package is talking to the network is a bad sign or not, because what if it’s a web server \u2014 then it’s obviously going to need to do that!” said Aboukhadijeh. But having that visibility integrated into the software building process is what developers need to prevent a supply chain attack. “This isn’t some complicated AI or machine learning thing,” he said, speaking of his own product. “There’s no way to hide that a package runs an install script, it’s declared as part of the package. So why not raise that to a developer’s attention?”<\/p>\nSocket is still in its early days and enters a crowded market, but is already attracting investment. The early-stage startup has raised $4.6 million in seed round funding from over a dozen angel investors and security leaders, including ex-GitHub CEO Nat Friedman, Keybase co-founder Max Krohn, as well as Unusual Ventures, Village Global and South Park Commons.<\/p>\nAboukhadijeh told TechCrunch that the funding will help grow the startup’s engineering, security analysis and research teams to build out its tools to developers.<\/p>\nRead more:<\/b><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/12\/17\/fireeye-breach-solarwinds-federal-agencies\/\">Just how bad is that hack that hit US government agencies?<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/11\/why-are-internet-asset-startups-so-hot-right-now\/\">Why are cybersecurity asset management startups so hot right now?<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/05\/kaseya-hack-flood-ransomware\/\">Kaseya hack floods hundreds of companies with ransomware<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Securing the software supply chain is admittedly somewhat of a dry topic, but knowing which components and code go into your everyday devices and appliances is a critical part of the software development process that billions of people rely on every day. Software is just like any other product you build and ship; it relies support"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/13\/new-york-geofence-keyword-search-warrants-bill\/\">for a New York bill<\/a> that would ban the use of controversial search warrants that can identify people based on their location data and internet search keywords.<\/p>\nIn"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/06\/18\/house-lawmakers-demand-end-to-warrantless-surveillance\/\">a brief statement<\/a>, the coalition known as Reform Government Surveillance said it “supports the adoption of New York Assembly Bill A84A, the Reverse Location Search Prohibition Act, which would prohibit the use of reverse location and reverse keyword searches.”<\/p>\nThe bill, if passed, would become the first state law to ban so-called geofence warrants and keyword search warrants, which rely on demanding tech companies turn over data about users who were near the scene of a crime or searched for particular keywords at a specific point in time. But the bill hasn’t moved since it was referred to a committee for discussion in January, the first major hurdle before it can be considered for a floor vote.<\/p>\nReform Government Surveillance was set up in 2013 by several Silicon Valley tech companies to lobby lawmakers for"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/06\/18\/house-lawmakers-demand-end-to-warrantless-surveillance\/\">reforms to U.S. surveillance laws<\/a> following the leak of classified documents by NSA contractor Edward Snowden. The coalition is now at 11 members \u2014 Amazon, Apple, Dropbox, Evernote, Google, Meta, Microsoft, Snap, Twitter, Yahoo (which owns TechCrunch) and Zoom.<\/p>\nThe move by the tech coalition to back the New York bill is not entirely altruistic. At least three of RGS’ members \u2014 Google, Microsoft and Yahoo \u2014 are frequently tapped by law enforcement for location data and search records of users because of the vast amount of data they store on billions of users around the world. Last year Google said that"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/08\/19\/google-geofence-warrants\/\">about one-quarter of all its U.S. legal demands are geofence warrants<\/a>, a figure that’s growing exponentially each year.<\/p>\nGeofence warrants are requested by law enforcement and signed by a judge to order companies like Google, Microsoft and Yahoo, which collect and store billions of location data points from its users’ phones and apps, to turn over location data on phones that were in"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/02\/06\/minneapolis-protests-geofence-warrant\/\">a certain geographic area<\/a> at a certain time to help identify suspects. Keyword search warrants work in a similar way, except a judge orders a search giant like Google to turn over user records of who"https:\/\/www.cnet.com\/tech\/services-and-software\/google-is-giving-data-to-police-based-on-search-keywords-court-docs-show\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">searched for certain keywords<\/a> during a particular moment in time.<\/p>\nCritics say these kinds of warrants are unconstitutional and can include data on individuals with no connection to a crime whatsoever, and in some cases can be accused of wrongdoing simply by being physically close to the scene of a crime.<\/p>\nThe ACLU, which"https:\/\/www.nyclu.org\/en\/campaigns\/end-dragnet-warrants-trap-innocent-new-yorkers\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">supports<\/a> the passage of the bill, told TechCrunch that it has seen thousands of people call on state lawmakers to pass the bill into law after launching an email campaign last week.<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"SNHT8ccPAm\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/13\/new-york-geofence-keyword-search-warrants-bill\/\">A bill to ban geofence and keyword search warrants in New York gains traction<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; York","slug":"new-york","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/4614"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=4614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=4614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=4614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=4614"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2312161,"date":"2022-05-05T10:00:15","date_gmt":"2022-05-05T17:00:15","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2312161"},"modified":"2022-05-05T11:41:10","modified_gmt":"2022-05-05T18:41:10","slug":"workrise-api-personal-information","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/05\/05\/workrise-api-personal-information\/","title":{"rendered":"Workrise fixes API that spilled users’ personal information"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Workforce management unicorn Workrise has fixed an exposed API that was spilling some users’ personal information.<\/p>\nThe Austin, Texas-based startup, which previously went by RigUp, was founded in 2014 as a marketplace for on-demand and skilled labor in the oil and gas industry. The company changed its name to Workrise in February 2021 to accommodate a broader set of energy sectors, like solar, construction and defense. By May 2021, Workrise said it had"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/20\/workrise-once-known-as-rigup-raises-300m-at-a-2-9b-valuation\/\">raised $300 million at a $2.9 billion valuation<\/a>. But last month,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/05\/workrise-cuts-staff-verticals-after-being-valued-at-2-9b-last-year\/\">Workrise announced layoffs<\/a> that reportedly hit hundreds of the company’s 600 employees after the midpandemic pivot failed to pan out.<\/p>\nNow, a security researcher who goes by the handle"https:\/\/twitter.com\/0xRzlr\">Rzlr<\/a> told TechCrunch that they found an exposed Workrise API that allowed anyone to retrieve personal information about subcontractors directly from Workrise servers without needing a password.<\/p>\nThe API was able to return names, email addresses and some employment details about subcontractor’s work, and names and email addresses about the people who provided references for the subcontractors, such as their former colleagues and managers.<\/p>\nIn simple terms, an API allows two things to talk with each other over the internet, like a smartphone app, a"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/05\/peloton-bug-account-data-leak\/\">Peloton bike<\/a>\u00a0or"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/03\/cbord-university-digital-locks\/\">door locks<\/a> that need to communicate with their servers. In this case the unauthenticated API could be queried using a web browser by plugging in a unique four-digit user ID that corresponds with a subcontractor’s review. But the user IDs were sequential, allowing anyone to access another subcontractor’s information simply by changing the user ID by a single digit, a common security flaw known as an insecure direct object reference bug \u2014 though Rzlr said not every digit returned a valid response.<\/p>\nSeveral of the exposed records seen by TechCrunch were created as far back as 2019 and marked as “draft.”<\/p>\nRzlr said in their limited testing of 1,000 records, they found more than 920 records with names and email addresses. Rzlr said the API did not limit the amount of data that could be downloaded, which they warned could have presented a scraping risk.<\/p>\nA screenshot shared with TechCrunch showed that the data could be easily scraped.<\/p>\nTechCrunch emailed CEO Xuan Yong and COO Mike Witte, who did not respond, but a short time later the API was no longer publicly accessible and was protected by a login page. In an emailed response, Eric Murphy, Workrise’s vice president of security, told TechCrunch: “Users maintain public profiles by default,” said Murphy. “To the extent Workrise determines any active user data was exposed that was not intended to be public, Workrise plans to notify those users directly.”<\/p>\nRzlr said they contacted several Workrise email addresses on April 22 \u2014 including Murphy’s and the company’s main security email address \u2014 about the exposed API. When asked why the API was not secured for two weeks until TechCrunch contacted the company, Murphy said the researcher’s emails were marked as spam.<\/p>\nWorkrise also fixed a second API issue that allowed anyone to obtain users’ referral codes, which could then be used to query the API to obtain the name, email address, phone number and the referral payment amount of users who invited others to join the site.<\/p>\nWhen asked if the company had carried out security audits of its systems, Murphy said the company had undergone “multiple” third-party audits but declined to name the company that allegedly performed them.<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"SbRyeSFpKA\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/05\/workrise-cuts-staff-verticals-after-being-valued-at-2-9b-last-year\/\">Workrise cuts staff, verticals after being valued at $2.9B last year<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Workrise cuts staff, verticals after being valued at $2.9B last year” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/05\/workrise-cuts-staff-verticals-after-being-valued-at-2-9b-last-year\/embed\/#?secret=B0RcBW4W6X#?secret=SbRyeSFpKA\" data-secret=\"SbRyeSFpKA\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Workforce management unicorn Workrise has fixed an exposed API that was spilling some users’ personal information. The Austin, Texas-based startup, which previously went by RigUp, was founded in 2014 as a marketplace for on-demand and skilled labor in the oil and gas industry. The company changed its name to Workrise in February 2021 to accommodate startup myNurse to shut down after data breach exposed health records"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">myNurse, a healthcare startup that provides chronic care management and remote patient monitoring services, said it will shut down at the end of the month after reporting a data breach that exposed personal health information of its users.<\/p>\nThe startup, which launched"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2018\/04\/18\/heres-the-23rd-batch-of-500-startups-companies\/\">as Salusive Health<\/a>, said in"https:\/\/www.documentcloud.org\/documents\/21832873-salusivemynurse-data-breach-notification\">a data breach notice<\/a> filed with the California attorney general’s office that it discovered a breach on March 7 during which an unauthorized individual accessed the company’s protected health data. The data breach notice warned that patients’ demographic, health, and financial information was accessed, including names, phone numbers, dates of birth, but also medical histories, diagnoses, treatments, lab test results, prescriptions, and health insurance information.<\/p>\nmyNurse said in the data breach notice that its decision to shutter its business “is unrelated to the data security incident,” but did not provide a reason for the unexpected shutdown. The company said it began notifying affected patients on April 29, the same date as its data breach notification, more than seven weeks after the breach was discovered.<\/p>\nmyNurse co-founder and chief executive Waleed Mohsen provided TechCrunch with a short statement saying the company was considering “how best to adjust our business model amid a changing healthcare landscape,” but declined to answer any of our questions about the data breach, including why it took the company seven weeks to notify affected patients or if myNurse had carried out a third-party security audit of its systems prior to the breach.<\/p>\nMohsen also declined to say how many patients are affected in total. Under the law of California, where myNurse is headquartered, companies must notify the attorney general’s office if more than 500 people are affected.<\/p>\nRead more:<\/b><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/03\/17\/medical-health-data-leak\/\">A huge trove of medical records and prescriptions found exposed<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/12\/01\/ntreatment-lab-results-medical-records-exposed\/\">Thousands of US lab results and medical records spilled online after a security lapse<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/01\/one-medical-exposed-customers-email-addresses\/\">An email sent by One Medical exposed hundreds of customers\u2019 email addresses<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/01\/28\/labcorp-website-bug-medical-data-exposed\/\">LabCorp security lapse exposed thousands of medical documents<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"myNurse, a healthcare startup that provides chronic care management and remote patient monitoring services, said it will shut down at the end of the month after reporting a data breach that exposed personal health information of its users. startup","slug":"healthcare-startup","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/71186958"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=71186958"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=71186958"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=71186958"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=71186958"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":801,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/security\/","name":"security","slug":"security","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/801"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=801"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=801"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2310324,"date":"2022-05-02T09:30:56","date_gmt":"2022-05-02T16:30:56","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2310324"},"modified":"2022-05-02T10:32:58","modified_gmt":"2022-05-02T17:32:58","slug":"remove-your-personal-data-from-google-search-cybersecurity-101","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/05\/02\/remove-your-personal-data-from-google-search-cybersecurity-101\/","title":{"rendered":"How to remove your personal information from Google search results"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Once something is on the internet, it’s almost impossible to take it down. But Google"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/28\/google-request-removal-personal-contact-information-search-results\/\">now makes it easier<\/a> for anyone to request the removal of their personal information from Google’s own search results.<\/p>\nGoogle has been"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/09\/24\/europes-top-court-rules-that-right-to-be-forgotten-only-applies-in-europe\/\">broadly against a global “right to be forgotten,”<\/a> a facet of European law that allows EU citizens to demand that companies, including tech giants, delete personal data that is stored about them. But that hasn’t stopped Google from giving users the tools to"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/28\/googles-user-controls-limit-ads-about-weight-loss-parenting\/\">limit what information it collects as a company<\/a>, as well as tools to let children and young adults"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/10\/27\/how-to-delete-your-kids-pictures-google-search\/\">request the removal of their photos<\/a> from Google’s image search results.<\/p>\nNow with its"https:\/\/support.google.com\/websearch\/troubleshooter\/9685456\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">new search results removal request feature<\/a>, you can ask Google to remove personally identifiable information about yourself (or someone you represent) from its search results, like a phone number, email address or your home address, and other sensitive information that could be used to break into your online accounts, like login credentials. Google said this can help victims of doxxing, where information about a person is published online without their consent, often with malicious intent.<\/p>\n"attachment_2310328\" style=\"width: 810px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\">"caption-attachment-2310328\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2310328\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg\" alt=\"Google request to remove your personal information off of Google search\" width=\"800\" height=\"652\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg 1484w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg?resize=150,122 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg?resize=300,245 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg?resize=768,626 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg?resize=680,554 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/05\/request-google-removal.jpg?resize=50,41 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 800px) 100vw, 800px\" \/>"caption-attachment-2310328\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Google lets you request to remove your personal information from its search results.
Image:<\/b> TechCrunch<\/p><\/div><\/p>\nTo request to remove your search results, first note"https:\/\/support.google.com\/websearch\/answer\/9673730\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">what Google needs from you<\/a> in order to process the removal request. Google explains what kinds of personal information it will remove, such as confidential government ID identification numbers, bank account numbers, images of ID documents, as well as contact information, like phone numbers and email addresses.<\/p>\nWhen you’re ready, head to"https:\/\/support.google.com\/websearch\/troubleshooter\/9685456\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Google’s search results removal form<\/a> and hitRemove information you see in Google Search<\/b>, thenIn<\/strong>Google’s search results and on a website<\/b>. At this point, Google will give you guidance if you want to reach out to the website host, but otherwise you can sayNo, I prefer not to<\/b> contact the website host and just move on with the form.<\/p>\nAt this point Google should ask you which search results you want removed. You should selectPersonal info, like ID numbers and private documents<\/b>, then select the kind of personal information that you want removed, such as your contact information, private medical documents or other government identity documents. From here, you should fill out the form with your corresponding information, including your full name, country of residence, your best contact email address and the list of web addresses of your personal information. You can grab the web addresses of infringing search results from your browser’s address bar.<\/p>\nThe final question on this form is if your personal information pertains to doxxing, which Google asks if the information is “being shared with malicious, threatening, or harassing intent.” If this is the case, selectYes<\/b>.<\/p>\nYou will have to provide all of the web addresses where you have found your information. That can mean both the web address of the webpage that has your information\u2014 and<\/i> the web addresses of the Google search results page. You may also have to"https:\/\/support.google.com\/adwords\/answer\/100070\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">take and submit screenshots<\/a> of search results to help Google narrow down the request to you. You can submit up to 1,000 web addresses at a time.<\/p>\nOnce you’re done, check the signature form at the bottom of the form.<\/p>\nRemember, removing your information from Google’s search results is not a panacea. Google’s request feature will not automatically remove your personal information from the websites your data is hosted on, but it makes it more difficult for others to discover the data without the aid of Google’s search algorithm.<\/p>\nFilling out the search removal form is also not a guarantee that Google will take action on your behalf. Google"https:\/\/blog.google\/products\/search\/new-options-for-removing-your-personally-identifiable-information-from-search\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">said<\/a> it will “evaluate all content on the web page to ensure that we’re not limiting the availability of other information that is broadly useful, for instance in news articles.” In cases where your information appears part of a public record, such as a court filing or a government website, Google said it wouldn’t remove those search results.<\/p>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/cybersecurity-101\/\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2018\/12\/cybersecurity-101.png\" alt=\"Cybersecurity 101 - TechCrunch\" \/><\/a><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Cybersecurity 101: It’s now easier to request the removal of your personal information from Google search offers bounty for Sandworm, the Russian hackers blamed for destructive cyberattacks"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">The U.S. government has stepped up its hunt for six Russian intelligence officers, best known as"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2018\/10\/04\/uk-says-russias-gru-was-behind-a-spate-of-chaotic-cyber-attacks-between-2015-and-2017\/\">the state-backed hacking group dubbed “Sandworm,”<\/a> by offering"https:\/\/www.state.gov\/rewards-for-justice-reward-offer-for-information-on-russian-military-intelligence-officers-conducting-malicious-activity-against-u-s-critical-infrastructure\/\">a $10 million bounty<\/a> for information that identifies or locates its members.<\/p>\nThe Sandworm hackers \u2014 who work for a division of Russia’s GRU, the country’s military intelligence division \u2014 are known for launching damaging and destructive cyberattacks against critical infrastructure, including food supplies and the energy sector.<\/p>\nSandworm may be best known for the NotPetya ransomware attack in 2017, which"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/10\/19\/justice-department-russian-hackers-notpetya-ukraine\/\">primarily hit computer systems in Ukraine<\/a> and disrupted the country’s power grid, leaving hundreds of thousands of residents without electricity during the depths of winter. In 2020, U.S. prosecutors"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/10\/19\/justice-department-russian-hackers-notpetya-ukraine\/\">indicted the same six Sandworm hackers<\/a>, who are believed to still be in Russia, for the NotPetya attack, as well as several other attacks that targeted the 2018 PyeongChang Winter Olympics in South Korea and for running a hack-and-leak operation to discredit France’s then-presidential frontrunner Emmanuel Macron.<\/p>\nIn a statement this week, the U.S. State Department said the NotPetya attack spilled outside of Ukraine across the wider internet, resulting in close to $1 billion in losses to the U.S. private sector, including medical facilities and hospitals.<\/p>\n\t"article-block block--callout block--right\" >\n\t\t"block--callout__title\">Read more<\/h4>\n\t\t\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/06\/fbi-operation-botnet-sandworm\/\">FBI operation aims to take down massive Russian GRU botnet<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/12\/ukraine-disrupts-attempt-by-russian-hackers-to-take-down-energy-provider\/\">Ukraine disrupts attempt by Russian hackers to take down energy provider<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/31\/viasat-cyberattack-russian-wiper\/\">Viasat cyberattack blamed on Russian wiper malware<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n\t<\/div>\n\t<\/p>\nThe timing of the bounty comes as U.S. officials warn that Russia-backed hackers, including Sandworm, could be"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/18\/cisa-fbi-satellite-networks\/\">preparing damaging cyberattacks<\/a> that target businesses and organizations in the United States following Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.<\/p>\nSince the start of the invasion in February, security researchers have attributed several cyberattacks to Sandworm, including the use of"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/31\/viasat-cyberattack-russian-wiper\/\">“wiper” malware to degrade Viasat’s satellite network<\/a> that the Ukrainian military heavily relies on. Ukraine’s government said earlier this month it had disrupted"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/12\/ukraine-disrupts-attempt-by-russian-hackers-to-take-down-energy-provider\/\">another Sandworm attempt to target a Ukrainian energy provider<\/a> using malware it repurposed from cyberattacks it launched against Ukraine in 2016.<\/p>\nThe FBI also this month said it conducted an operation to disrupt"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/06\/fbi-operation-botnet-sandworm\/\">a massive botnet that infected thousands of compromised routers<\/a>, including many located in the U.S., by locking the Sandworm hackers out about half of the botnet’s command and control servers.<\/p>\nSandworm is also blamed for several other destructive cyberattacks in Ukraine, according to new research from Microsoft, as part of the group’s efforts to support Russian military objectives by degrading Ukraine’s economy.<\/p>\nMicrosoft said that Sandworm, which it calls “Iridium” as part of its metal-themed convention of naming cyber adversaries, also launched a destructive attack on the network of a transportation and logistics provider in Western Ukraine, which the company said may have been to hamper Ukraine’s efforts to supply the bulk of military equipment and humanitarian assistance entering the country to conflict zones in the country’s east.<\/p>\nThe technology giant also warned that Sandworm \u2014 and Fancy Bear, another GRU hacking unit known as Fancy Bear \u2014 continues to pursue companies that support the communications sector and an unnamed “major” internet provider. Microsoft did not say which internet provider but warned that the activity was detected as recently as this month. The Ukrainian government said last month"https:\/\/twitter.com\/dsszzi\/status\/1508528209075257347\">it had “neutralized”<\/a> a cyberattack targeting the IT infrastructure of Ukrtelecom, the country’s largest internet provider.<\/p>\nTom Burt, Microsoft’s consumer security chief, said the company has observed close to 40 destructive attacks directly targeting critical infrastructure, with about 40% of those attacks “aimed at organizations in critical infrastructure sectors that could have negative second-order effects on the Ukrainian government, military, economy and people.”<\/p>\nNot all of the attacks were successful. In one case, Microsoft said it found evidence that Sandworm was setting the stage for a file-encrypting attack on an agriculture firm, likely to disrupt its grain production supply, for which Ukraine is a major global exporter.<\/p>\nSandworm and Fancy Bear are two of six separate Russian state-run hacking groups targeting Ukraine in more than 237 operations since just before the invasion, Burt said.<\/p>\n“The attacks have not only degraded the systems of institutions in Ukraine but have also sought to disrupt people\u2019s access to reliable information and critical life services, and have attempted to shake confidence in the country\u2019s leadership,” said Burt. “We have also observed limited espionage attack activity involving other NATO member states, and some disinformation activity.”<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"7OKDdaPzdP\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/02\/ukraine-russia-us-cybersecurity\/\">How the conflict in Ukraine threatens US cybersecurity<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“How the conflict in Ukraine threatens US cybersecurity” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/02\/ukraine-russia-us-cybersecurity\/embed\/#?secret=LsqWCl14P5#?secret=7OKDdaPzdP\" data-secret=\"7OKDdaPzdP\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"The U.S. government has stepped up its hunt for six Russian intelligence officers, best known as the state-backed hacking group dubbed “Sandworm,” by offering a $10 million bounty for information that identifies or locates its members. The Sandworm hackers \u2014 who work for a division of Russia’s GRU, the country’s military intelligence division \u2014 are Investigation","slug":"federal-bureau-of-investigation","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/231892"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=231892"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=231892"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=231892"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=231892"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":576609133,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/gru\/","name":"GRU","slug":"gru","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/576609133"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=576609133"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=576609133"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=576609133"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=576609133"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":22,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/internet\/","name":"Internet","slug":"internet","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/22"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=22"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=22"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=22"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=22"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2301873,"date":"2022-04-18T12:16:57","date_gmt":"2022-04-18T19:16:57","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2301873"},"modified":"2022-04-18T14:55:33","modified_gmt":"2022-04-18T21:55:33","slug":"web-scraping-legal-court","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/18\/web-scraping-legal-court\/","title":{"rendered":"Web scraping is legal, US appeals court reaffirms"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Good news for archivists, academics, researchers and journalists: Scraping publicly accessible data is legal, according to a U.S. appeals court ruling.<\/p>\nThe landmark ruling by the U.S. Ninth Circuit of Appeals is the latest in a long-running legal battle brought by LinkedIn aimed at stopping a rival company from web scraping personal information from users’ public profiles. The case"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/14\/supreme-court-revives-linkedin-bid-to-protect-user-data-from-web-scrapers\/\">reached the U.S. Supreme Court<\/a> last year but was sent back to the Ninth Circuit for the original appeals court to re-review the case.<\/p>\nIn its second ruling on Monday, the Ninth Circuit reaffirmed its original decision and found that scraping data that is publicly accessible on the internet is not a violation of"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/11\/29\/supreme-court-van-buren-hacking\/\">the Computer Fraud and Abuse Act<\/a>, or CFAA, which governs what constitutes computer hacking under U.S. law.<\/p>\nThe Ninth Circuit’s decision is a major win for archivists, academics, researchers and journalists who use tools to mass collect, or scrape, information that is publicly accessible on the internet. Without a ruling in place, long-running projects to archive websites no longer online and using publicly accessible data for academic and research studies have been left in legal limbo.<\/p>\nBut there have been egregious cases of web scraping that have sparked privacy and security concerns. Facial recognition startup Clearview AI claims to have scraped billions of social media profile photos, prompting"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/04\/21\/data-brokers-bill-wyden-paul-privacy-clearview\/\">several tech giants to file lawsuits<\/a> against the startup. Several companies, including"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/09\/04\/facebook-phone-numbers-exposed\/\">Facebook<\/a>, Instagram,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/01\/11\/scraped-parler-data-is-a-metadata-goldmine\/\">Parler<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/06\/16\/millions-venmo-transactions-scraped\/\">Venmo<\/a>\u00a0and Clubhouse have all had users’ data scraped over the years.<\/p>\nThe case before the Ninth Circuit was originally brought by LinkedIn against Hiq Labs, a company that uses public data to analyze employee attrition. LinkedIn said Hiq’s mass web scraping of LinkedIn user profiles was against its terms of service, amounted to hacking and was therefore a violation of the CFAA. LinkedIn first lost"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2016\/08\/15\/linkedin-sues-scrapers\/\">the case against Hiq<\/a> in 2019 after the Ninth Circuit found that the CFAA does not bar anyone from scraping data that’s publicly accessible.<\/p>\nOn its second pass of the case, the Ninth Circuit said it relied on"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/03\/supreme-court-hacking-cfaa-ruling\/\">a Supreme Court decision<\/a> last June, during which the U.S. top court took its first look at the decades-old CFAA. In its ruling, the Supreme Court narrowed what constitutes a violation of the CFAA as those who gain unauthorized access to a computer system \u2014 rather than a broader interpretation of exceeding existing authorization, which the court argued could have attached criminal penalties to “a breathtaking amount of commonplace computer activity.” Using a “gate-up, gate-down” analogy, the Supreme Court said that when a computer or website’s gates are up \u2014 and therefore information is publicly accessible \u2014 no authorization is required.<\/p>\nThe Ninth Circuit, in referencing the Supreme Court’s “gate-up, gate-down” analogy, ruled that “the concept of ‘without authorization’ does not apply to public websites.”<\/p>\n“We\u2019re disappointed in the court\u2019s decision. This is a preliminary ruling and the case is far from over,” said LinkedIn spokesperson Greg Snapper in a statement. “We will continue to fight to protect our members\u2019 ability to control the information they make available on LinkedIn. When your data is taken without permission and used in ways you haven\u2019t agreed to, that\u2019s not okay. On LinkedIn, our members trust us with their information, which is why we prohibit unauthorized scraping on our platform.”<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"UiGWNhmGsA\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/03\/supreme-court-hacking-cfaa-ruling\/\">Supreme Court limits US hacking law in landmark CFAA ruling<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Supreme Court limits US hacking law in landmark CFAA ruling” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/03\/supreme-court-hacking-cfaa-ruling\/embed\/#?secret=Kgp97JGgVy#?secret=UiGWNhmGsA\" data-secret=\"UiGWNhmGsA\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Good news for archivists, academics, researchers and journalists: Scraping publicly accessible data is legal, according to a U.S. appeals court ruling. The landmark ruling by the U.S. Ninth Circuit of Appeals is the latest in a long-running legal battle brought by LinkedIn aimed at stopping a rival company from web scraping personal information from States","slug":"united-states","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/449557049"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=449557049"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=449557049"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=449557049"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=449557049"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":30151228,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/venmo\/","name":"Venmo","slug":"venmo","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/30151228"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=30151228"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=30151228"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=30151228"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=30151228"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2299634,"date":"2022-04-13T09:20:47","date_gmt":"2022-04-13T16:20:47","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2299634"},"modified":"2022-04-13T11:09:11","modified_gmt":"2022-04-13T18:09:11","slug":"xinjiang-prisoner-hikvision-china","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/13\/xinjiang-prisoner-hikvision-china\/","title":{"rendered":"‘Always on and watching’: A former Xinjiang prisoner describes life inside China’s detention camps"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">"featured__span-first-words\">For 10 months<\/span> in 2018, Ovalbek Turdakun was a prisoner in one of China’s notorious detention camps, where he was tortured, subject to horrific conditions and under constant surveillance.<\/p>\nIn a makeshift courtroom inside the detention camp he was being held, Turdakun was not permitted to speak and was made to sign papers he was given no time to read. As a former law student, he knew that the court was not following a proper legal process, but was nevertheless told that the court’s decision would lead to “great things” for him and that he would study and live for free.<\/p>\nTurdakun is a Chinese passport holder and an ethnic Kyrgyz, one of several ethnic groups \u2014 including Kazakhs, Tajiks and Uyghurs \u2014 that have been charged with dubious if not invented charges and detained in vast detention camps across Xinjiang, a region in northwestern China where most of the ethnic groups live. Beijing calls them vocational and education centers and says they are for combating Islamic extremism. But Turdakun is a Christian, who"https:\/\/www.theglobeandmail.com\/world\/article-in-xinjiang-incarceration-of-christians-and-han-chinese-shows-broad\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">researchers say<\/a> have also been targeted and arbitrarily detained by the state.<\/p>\nUnited Nations watchdogs say China has incarcerated at least a million of its own citizens in detention camps in recent years, but the figure is believed to be higher. The"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/01\/federal-lorex-surveillance-ban\/\">Biden administration<\/a> declared China’s treatment of Uyghurs and other Muslim minorities a “genocide,” though Beijing has long denied allegations of human rights abuses.<\/p>\nTurdakun is only able to tell this story after U.S. immigration authorities granted him and his family"https:\/\/www.uscis.gov\/humanitarian\/humanitarian-or-significant-public-benefit-parole-for-individuals-outside-the-united-states\">advanced parole<\/a>, a temporary immigration status allowing them to enter the United States, after congressional lawmakers lobbied on their behalf. Turdakun and his wife, Zhyldyz Uraalieva, and their son arrived in Washington, D.C., on April 8.<\/p>\n“There is no freedom inside that place,” Turdakun said in an interview with TechCrunch, speaking through a translator, in Washington on Tuesday. Even after he was released under conditions similar to that of house arrest, Turdakun said he would be seen on facial recognition and harassed by police officers every time he left the house.<\/p>\n"attachment_2299931\" style=\"width: 410px\" class=\"wp-caption alignright\">"caption-attachment-2299931\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2299931\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?w=565\" alt=\"\" width=\"400\" height=\"481\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg 898w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?resize=125,150 125w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?resize=249,300 249w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?resize=768,924 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?resize=565,680 565w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/exif-photo-conor.jpg?resize=42,50 42w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px\" \/>"caption-attachment-2299931\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">IPVM government director Conor Healy (left), Ovalbek Turdakun, his son, and wife Zhyldyz (right) in Kyrgyzstan, taken before they arrived in the United States in April.
Image Credits:<\/strong> Conor Healy\/provided<\/p><\/div><\/p>\nAs a former prisoner, Turdakun is one of only a few people with a firsthand account of the inside of China’s detention camps, including rare knowledge of how the Chinese government uses technology, surveillance and facial recognition to oppress millions of Xinjiang residents, which U.S. lawmakers will use to investigate human rights abuses in China and the Chinese companies that supply surveillance technology to the camps.<\/p>\nA letter seen by TechCrunch that was sent by Rep. Chris Smith of New Jersey in support of Turdakun’s advance parole case said his knowledge will provide vital evidence regarding the “use of technology provided by Chinese companies such as Hikvision to facilitate gross violations of internationally recognized human rights by the Chinese government.”<\/p>\nSmith, whose office did not return a request for comment, is a vocal critic in Congress of China’s human rights record, including its use of surveillance technology for carrying out human rights violations. Sen. Marco Rubio, vice-chair of the Senate Intelligence Committee, also"https:\/\/www.axios.com\/xinjiang-china-camps-human-rights-abuse-witness-3c2de155-0f9a-4f2b-84e1-83713bb7e443.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">reportedly<\/a> supported Turdakun’s immigration effort.<\/p>\nHikvision is one of the world’s largest suppliers of video surveillance cameras, making about $10 billion in profit in 2020. A year earlier, it was one of several Chinese technology companies"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/10\/07\/eight-chinese-tech-firms-placed-on-u-s-entity-list-for-their-role-in-human-rights-violations-against-muslim-minority-groups\/\">added<\/a> to the U.S. government\u2019s economic sanctions entity list, effectively barring the company from buying U.S. components without the government’s approval, citing its role in"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/10\/07\/eight-chinese-tech-firms-placed-on-u-s-entity-list-for-their-role-in-human-rights-violations-against-muslim-minority-groups\/\">enabling human rights abuses<\/a> in Xinjiang.<\/p>\nChiefly, successive U.S. administrations have alleged that Beijing relies heavily on companies like"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/24\/united-states-towns-hikvision-dahua-surveillance\/\">Hikvision<\/a>, but also"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/01\/federal-lorex-surveillance-ban\/\">Dahua<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/05\/15\/us-blacklist-huawei-70-affiliates\/\"> Huawei<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/12\/chinas-ai-giant-sensetime-postpones-ipo-after-us-blacklisting\/\">SenseTime<\/a> and others, to supply the surveillance technology it uses to monitor the Xinjiang population both across the region and also in its many detention camps.<\/p>\nAhead of arriving in the United States, Turdakun described the conditions of his detention, brutal interrogations and forced medical procedures in a series of video interviews recorded by Conor Healy, government director at video surveillance news site"https:\/\/ipvm.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">IPVM<\/a>. In December, Healy met Turdakun and his family in Kyrgyzstan where they had been for the past year, to help them obtain their immigration paperwork onward to the United States, amid fears that the Kyrgyz authorities could deport the family back to China, Healy told TechCrunch.<\/p>\nIn one of the video interviews shared with TechCrunch, Healy showed Turdakun a photo of Hikvision’s logo, which the former prisoner immediately recognized, saying that it was the same logo on the cameras in the cells in the detention camp and littered throughout the city.<\/p>\nSpeaking with TechCrunch on Tuesday, Turdakun described the cells where he would be held with two dozen other prisoners for months at a time and how the cameras, all branded with Hikvision logos, were “always on and watching,” he said. If the cameras saw anyone speak, a booming voice would tell them not to talk.<\/p>\nHe described how the detainees would spend hours in silence, enforced by the cameras, and they would have little other human contact outside of the cells for extended periods; often the door would stay shut for long timespans and food would be pushed through a slot in the door. Even to move just a few feet to use the hole-shaped toilet that he described, you would still need to raise your hand and ask for permission “because of the cameras watching, always,” Turdakun said.<\/p>\nTurdakun was released in November 2018 on terms similar to house arrest, where he would be monitored around the clock from the GPS tracker on his wrist that could only be unlocked with a special key. Although he was allowed to leave his house and travel around his small city, he described constant harassment from the authorities.<\/p>\n“Every time,” he stressed when asked again.<\/p>\n“The cameras would see me and send off alarms,” he said, describing the use of facial recognition across his neighborhood. “The cameras are about [6 feet] in height \u2014 also Hikvision \u2014 and they’re on every sidewalk,” he said. “There are so many of them, they don’t need to change the directions of the cameras. It doesn’t matter how long the road is, even the shortest road will have cameras. The whole entire city has cameras watching.”<\/p>\nTechCrunch could not independently verify Turdakun’s account, which is"https:\/\/www.theguardian.com\/world\/2021\/sep\/30\/uyghur-tribunal-testimony-surveillance-china\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">consistent with other, albeit rare accounts<\/a> from survivors of Xinjiang detention camps. During the interview, Turdakun showed a sketch that he drew mapping the layout of the detention camp, which corresponds with satellite imagery of the camp where he was held.<\/p>\nIn an emailed statement sent via a public relations firm specializing in crisis management, Hikvision said it “takes all reports regarding human rights very seriously,” but declined to provide a company spokesperson’s name.<\/p>\nLiu Pengyu, a spokesperson for the Chinese Embassy in Washington, D.C., denied the allegations in an emailed statement.<\/p>\nHuman rights lawyers say the former prisoner’s testimony will provide important evidence for"https:\/\/tgchambers.com\/news-and-resources\/news\/new-evidence-submitted-to-the-icc-on-behalf-of-uyghurs\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">the case filed<\/a> with the International Criminal Court in The Hague. U.K. attorney Rodney Dixon, who is leading the team of lawyers compiling evidence of human rights abuses by China, said in a letter supporting Turdakun’s advance parole that it was “vital” for him to testify in future proceedings.<\/p>\nTurdakun told TechCrunch that he wants more people to know about the conditions in Xinjiang.<\/p>\n“Coming to America and being at peace and safe has been a goal for our family for a long time,” he said.<\/p>\nRead more:<\/strong><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/24\/united-states-towns-hikvision-dahua-surveillance\/\">US towns are buying Chinese surveillance tech tied to Uighur abuses<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/10\/07\/eight-chinese-tech-firms-placed-on-u-s-entity-list-for-their-role-in-human-rights-violations-against-muslim-minority-groups\/\">Chinese tech firms placed on sanctions list for human rights violations<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/01\/federal-lorex-surveillance-ban\/\">US government agencies bought Chinese surveillance tech despite federal ban<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/06\/03\/white-house-expands-investment-ban-on-chinese-tech-and-telecom-companies\/\">White House expands investment ban on Chinese tech and telecom companies<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"For 10 months in 2018, Ovalbek Turdakun was a prisoner in one of China’s notorious detention camps, where he was tortured, subject to horrific conditions and under constant surveillance. lands $2.3M to expand cybersecurity awareness training"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">If you work at a company above a certain size, you’ll understand just how little patience we all feel for internal phishing awareness, even despite the fact that phishing remains one of the leading causes of data breaches. HacWare, a New York-based startup and"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/09\/17\/meet-the-five-startup-battlefield-finalists-at-disrupt-2020\/\">TechCrunch Battlefield finalist<\/a>, tries to"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/09\/15\/hacware-email-security\/\">help employees hate phishing simulations a little less<\/a>, and it just landed $2.3 million in seed funding to expand its cybersecurity awareness and training offering.<\/p>\nThe idea behind HacWare is simple enough: It’s a service that integrates with your company’s Google Workspace or Office 365 and helps employees understand potential risks in incoming email and automates phishing simulations and cybersecurity training.<\/p>\nHacWare aims to give companies an easier onramp to provide meaningful and engaging cybersecurity training to their employees that they won’t hate or that gets in the way of their day-to-day work. The goal is to give employees the skills to protect their company from a growing range of inbound threats, many of which initially target their inbox in an effort to steal their passwords.<\/p>\nTiffany Ricks, a former ethical hacker and HacWare founder, knows what it’s like to be a startup that has to do more with less. Starting out with just a handful of people on her team, Ricks told TechCrunch that she plans to use the seed round raise to build out the startup’s engineering and sales team to help protect companies from identity theft and phishing attacks.<\/p>\n“This funding will allow HacWare to execute our product strategy goals for making cybersecurity training super adaptive and seamless for global workforces. We are re-imaging workplace training to coach company employees on how to protect their identities and combat phishing attacks,” said Ricks.<\/p>\nSince HacWare’s"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/video\/hacware-email-hacking-protection\/\">Battlefield appearance in 2020<\/a>, held virtually because of the COVID-19 pandemic, Ricks said the company paused its seed-round fundraise, reinvested its revenues back into the company and re-focused on its product. Ricks said it was part of a promise to put out a new product feature every week for a year. “Initially, it seemed impossible but every week we were releasing a fully functional feature, and putting out video content explaining the features,” she said. Ricks said focusing on what its customers wanted helped attract investors back to the seed round she had put on hold.<\/p>"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\nThe seed round was led by Elevate Capital, with participation from 2.0 Ventures, Aura Innovation Fund, Chai Angels and others.<\/p>\nThe funding will also help expand the company’s developer network to offer more ways for companies to integrate their internal applications and HR platforms directly with HacWare’s API with just a few lines of code. Ricks said the developer network is a key component to the company’s product strategy, especially given the shift to remote work driven by the pandemic.<\/p>\nElevate Capital founder and managing partner Nitin Rai, who invested in the round, is joining HacWare’s board.<\/p>\nRicks notes that,"https:\/\/news.crunchbase.com\/news\/something-ventured-black-women-founders\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">according to Crunchbase<\/a>, less than 1% of all venture capital funding went to Black female founders. By her count, Ricks is the 114th Black female founder to raise over $1 million in venture funding.<\/p>\n“I hope I am inspiring more Black women to dream and build big scalable businesses,” said Ricks.<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"j3Sxc5JXPU\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/09\/15\/hacware-email-security\/\">HacWare wants you to hate email security a little less<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“HacWare wants you to hate email security a little less” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/09\/15\/hacware-email-security\/embed\/#?secret=NkkiTBlsub#?secret=j3Sxc5JXPU\" data-secret=\"j3Sxc5JXPU\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"If you work at a company above a certain size, you’ll understand just how little patience we all feel for internal phishing awareness, even despite the fact that phishing remains one of the leading causes of data breaches. HacWare, a New York-based startup and TechCrunch Battlefield finalist, tries to help employees hate phishing simulations a Equity","slug":"private-equity","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/29172"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=29172"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=29172"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=29172"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=29172"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2298529,"date":"2022-04-12T04:00:53","date_gmt":"2022-04-12T11:00:53","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2298529"},"modified":"2022-04-12T13:40:57","modified_gmt":"2022-04-12T20:40:57","slug":"aethon-robots-hospitals-hijacks","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/12\/aethon-robots-hospitals-hijacks\/","title":{"rendered":"Autonomous robots used in hundreds of hospitals at risk of remote hijacks"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">"featured__span-first-words\">A decade ago<\/span> security researcher Barnaby Jack famously wirelessly hacked a hospital insulin pump live on stage in front of hundreds of people to demonstrate how easily it could be compromised to deliver a lethal dose of medication. In the years that have passed, medical device security has gotten better, albeit with an"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/07\/09\/flaws-anesthesia-respiratory-devices-tampering\/\">occasional high-profile hiccup<\/a>. But researchers are now finding vulnerabilities in newer hospital technologies that weren’t as ubiquitous a decade ago.<\/p>\nEnter autonomous hospital robots, the supposed-to-be-friendly self-controlled digital workhorses that can transport medications, bed linens, food, medications and laboratory specimens across a hospital campus. These robots, such as the ones built by robot maker Aethon, are equipped with the space to transport critical goods and security access to enter restricted parts of the hospital and ride elevators, all while cutting labor costs.<\/p>\nBut researchers at Cynerio, a cybersecurity startup focused on securing hospital and healthcare systems, discovered a set of five never-before-seen vulnerabilities in Aethon robots, which they say allowed malicious hackers to remotely hijack and control these autonomous robots \u2014 and in some cases over the internet.<\/p>\nThe five vulnerabilities, which Cynerio collectively call JekyllBot:5, aren’t with the robots themselves but with the base servers that are used to communicate with and control the robots that traverse the hallways of the hospitals and hotels. The bugs range from allowing hackers to create new users with high-level access in order to then log in and remotely control the robots and access restricted areas, snoop on patients or guests using the robot’s in-built cameras or otherwise cause mayhem.<\/p>\nAsher Brass, the lead researcher on the Aethon vulnerabilities, warned that the flaws required a “very low skill set for exploitation.”<\/p>\n"attachment_2298553\" style=\"width: 1119px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\">"caption-attachment-2298553\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout wp-image-2298553 size-full\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1109\" height=\"624\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg 1109w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/04\/robot-moving-aethon-1.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1109px) 100vw, 1109px\" \/>"caption-attachment-2298553\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">A screenshot from one of the cameras on an Aethon TUG robot.
Image Credits:<\/strong> Cynerio<\/p><\/div><\/p>\nCynerio said the base servers have a web interface that could be accessed from inside the hospital’s network, allowing “guest” users to view real-time robot camera feeds and their upcoming schedules and tasks for the day without needing a password. But although the robots’ functionality were protected by an “admin” account, the researchers said the vulnerabilities in the web interface could have allowed a hacker to interact with the robots without needing an admin password to log in.<\/p>\nOne of the five bugs, the researchers said, exposed robots to remote control using a joystick-style controller in the web interface, while exploiting another one of the bugs to interact with door locks, call and ride elevators, and open and close medication drawers.<\/p>\nFor the most part, the potential risk is limited if access to the robots’ base servers are confined to the local network, limiting access only to logged-in employees. The researchers said the risk was far greater for the hospitals, hotels or any other place that use these robots that have a base server connected to the internet, since the vulnerabilities can be triggered from anywhere on the internet.<\/p>\nCynerio said they found evidence of internet-exposed robots in hospitals as well as facilities providing care to veterans. Aethon touts its robots in hundreds of hospitals around the world, many in the United States, accounting for thousands of robots.<\/p>\nThe bugs were fixed in a batch of software and firmware updates released by Aethon, after Cynerio alerted the company to the issues. Aethon is said to have restricted internet-exposed servers to isolate the robots from potential remote attacks, and fixed other web-related vulnerabilities that affected the base station.<\/p>\nIn a statement given to TechCrunch, CEO of ST Engineering Aethon, Peter Seiff, confirmed the vulnerabilities but declined to answer our questions, such as what percentage of its customers’ autonomous robots had been patched following the software update.<\/p>\nRead more:<\/strong><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/03\/cbord-university-digital-locks\/\">How a simple security bug became a university campus \u2018master key\u2019<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/07\/02\/smart-home-hub-flaws-unlock-doors\/\">Security flaws in a popular smart home hub let hackers unlock front doors<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/11\/why-are-internet-asset-startups-so-hot-right-now\/\">Why are cybersecurity asset management startups so hot right now?<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"A decade ago security researcher Barnaby Jack famously wirelessly hacked a hospital insulin pump live on stage in front of hundreds of people to demonstrate how easily it could be compromised to deliver a lethal dose of medication. In the years that have passed, medical device security has gotten better, albeit with an occasional high-profile probably pissed away $200 million,’ Better.com CEO told employees in layoffs meeting"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">"featured__span-first-words\">When Better.com CEO<\/span> Vishal Garg <\/span>"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/01\/better-com-lays-off-9-of-its-staff\/\">"font-weight: 400;\">laid off 900 employees<\/span><\/a>"font-weight: 400;\">, or about 9% of the company\u2019s staff, in early December, the startup world was shocked by his callous delivery.<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">Now a video of Garg and CFO Kevin Ryan addressing the remaining employees right after the chief executive performed those layoffs has emerged, confirming many reports of his brash style and harsh words about those affected.<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">In a video obtained by TechCrunch, Garg is seen addressing the layoffs and admitting to making a number of mistakes.<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">We chose not to publish the video in an effort to protect the identity of the source, but we\u2019ve picked out the most relevant bits based on a transcription of the 12-minute meeting here:<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">About two minutes into the meeting, Garg said: \u201c<\/span>"font-weight: 400;\">Make no mistake, we did also eliminate redundant roles \u2014 who might be strong performers but were in the wrong place at the wrong time, with the wrong task, and weren’t mission-critical.\u201d<\/span><\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"VgUF7d7R08\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/03\/better-com-teaches-us-how-not-to-conduct-mass-layoffs\/\">Better.com teaches us how not to downsize a company<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Better.com teaches us how not to downsize a company” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/03\/better-com-teaches-us-how-not-to-conduct-mass-layoffs\/embed\/#?secret=zzOEqhB3iB#?secret=VgUF7d7R08\" data-secret=\"VgUF7d7R08\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n"font-weight: 400;\">After about four minutes, Garg also acknowledged that the company was continuing to hire, including some interns, in the midst of the layoffs, while at the time making a thinly veiled threat: <\/span>"font-weight: 400;\">“… It’s because we expect those people to be super productive and add value, and if they don’t we will exit them, too.\u201d <\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">He added: <\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">“We are going to be leaner, meaner and hungrier going forward. We will not be spending time trying to raise capital. We will not be spending time focused on what investors think. We will be spending time grinding this business forward in what will likely be a bloodbath in the mortgage industry in the next year or two.”<\/span><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"font-weight: 400;\">In the video at around the eight-minute mark, Garg admitted to not being disciplined in managing the company\u2019s cash and in its hiring strategy, which helps explain the company\u2019s second mass layoff of over 3,000 people just three months later. It also helps support multiple sources\u2019 claims that the company is currently \u201closing $50 million per month.\u201d<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">“T<\/span>"font-weight: 400;\">oday we acknowledge that we overhired, and hired the wrong people. And in doing that we failed. I failed. I was not disciplined over the past 18 months. We made $250 million last year, and you know what, we probably pissed away $200 million. We probably could have made more money last year and been leaner, meaner and hungrier.”<\/span><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"font-weight: 400;\">He also explicitly said that the company lost $100 million in the previous quarter, saying it was his \u201cmistake\u201d for not laying off staff earlier.<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">“We should\u2019ve done what we did today three months ago \u2026 that\u2019s what I\u2019m saying to you right now, it\u2019s what I\u2019ve said to the board before. \u2026You will not be allowed to fail twice. You\u2019ll be encouraged to fail once, but not allowed to fail twice.\u201d<\/span><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"lWSsBTXLBI\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/06\/better-com-offering-employees-60-days-severance-losing-tens-of-millions-per-month-per-sources\/\">After mass layoffs, Better.com offers severance, health insurance to employees who voluntarily resign<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“After mass layoffs, Better.com offers severance, health insurance to employees who voluntarily resign” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/06\/better-com-offering-employees-60-days-severance-losing-tens-of-millions-per-month-per-sources\/embed\/#?secret=E04AMCVulH#?secret=lWSsBTXLBI\" data-secret=\"lWSsBTXLBI\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n"font-weight: 400;\">At the end of the video, Ryan takes over and addresses the employees\u00a0<\/span><\/p>\n"font-weight: 400;\">\u201cThere will be people who say that raising a lot of money and reducing headcount all in the same week are inconsistent \u2014 how could you do that? Well we think they’re entirely consistent. As Vishal said, thousands of startups start every year, thousands of startups fail every year. As a management team, we are always going to do what puts the company in the best place possible and that’s having a very strong balance sheet and a lean business that’s on the path to profitability. So this is all about offense, it’s darkest before the dawn, 2022 comes and it will be a lot of carnage in this industry and we’re going to capitalize \u2014 lean, mean, and now is our opportunity to grow and to grow even faster than we’ve been growing. So while this is hard, we’ll get through this, this too shall pass, and on to 2022.”<\/span><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"font-weight: 400;\">The video reveals what many former employees have described: leadership that comes across as arrogant in its approach, expressing a cutthroat attitude when it comes to layoffs, and admitting to mismanagement of financial and human capital.<\/span><\/p>\nWhen reached, an unnamed Better.com spokesperson did not dispute the authenticity of the recording or the CEO’s remarks and declined several times to provide their name for this story.<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"huFP4kL7mm\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/08\/fast-better-startup-2022\/\">Startups in 2022 are indeed \u2018harder, Better, Faster, stronger\u2019<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Startups in 2022 are indeed \u2018harder, Better, Faster, stronger\u2019” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/08\/fast-better-startup-2022\/embed\/#?secret=e4ETIrYSuL#?secret=huFP4kL7mm\" data-secret=\"huFP4kL7mm\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"When Better.com CEO Vishal Garg laid off 900 employees, or about 9% of the company\u2019s staff, in early December, the startup world was shocked by his callous delivery.
