operation aims to take down massive Russian GRU botnet"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">The Federal Bureau of Investigation has disclosed it carried out an operation in March to target a massive\u00a0botnet controlled by Russian intelligence.<\/p>\nThe operation was authorized by courts in California and Pennsylvania, allowing the FBI to copy and remove the so-called Cyclops Blink malware from its command and control servers, also known as C2s, allowing the FBI to sever the connections to thousands of compromised infected devices that were taking instructions from the servers.<\/p>\nThe Justice Department"https:\/\/www.justice.gov\/opa\/pr\/justice-department-announces-court-authorized-disruption-botnet-controlled-russian-federation\">announced the March operation on Wednesday<\/a>, describing it as “successful,” but warned that device owners should still review\u00a0the"https:\/\/www.ncsc.gov.uk\/news\/joint-advisory-shows-new-sandworm-malware-cyclops-blink-replaces-vpnfilter\">initial February 23 advisory<\/a> to secure their compromised devices and prevent reinfection.<\/p>\nThe Justice Department said that since the news first emerged about the rising threat of Cyclops Blink in February, thousands of compromised devices have been secured by the owners but justified"https:\/\/www.documentcloud.org\/documents\/21579373-doj-order-to-remove-cyclops-blink-malware\">its court-ordered operation<\/a> because the “majority” of infected devices were still compromised just weeks later in mid-March.<\/p>\nCyclops Blink is believed to be the successor to VPNFilter, a botnet largely neglected after it was exposed by security researchers in 2018 and later targeted by a U.S. government operation to disrupt its command and control servers. Both Cyclops Blink and VPNFilter are"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2018\/10\/04\/uk-says-russias-gru-was-behind-a-spate-of-chaotic-cyber-attacks-between-2015-and-2017\/\">attributed to Sandworm<\/a>, a group of hackers working for Russia’s GRU, the country’s military intelligence unit.<\/p>\nAccording to the Justice Department, the court order had the “immediate effect of preventing Sandworm from accessing these C2 devices, thereby disrupting Sandworm\u2019s control of the infected bot devices controlled by the remediated C2 devices.”<\/p>\n“The operation did not involve any FBI communications with bot devices,” the Justice Department said.<\/p>\nU.S. authorities did not speculate on the goal of the Cyclops Blink botnet, but security researchers"https:\/\/www.trendmicro.com\/en_us\/research\/22\/c\/cyclops-blink-sets-sights-on-asus-routers--.html\">say<\/a> the botnet is capable of collecting information and conducting espionage, launching distributed"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/11\/15\/cloudflare-terabits-ddos-attack\/\">denial-of-service attacks<\/a> that overload websites and servers with junk traffic, as well as destructive attacks that render the devices inoperable and causing system and network disruptions.<\/p>\nSandworm is particularly known for launching disruptive hacks over the years, including knocking the Ukrainian power grid offline, using malware to try to"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/25\/doj-russia-triton-united-states-nuclear\/\">blow up a Saudi petrochemical plant<\/a>, and more recently deploying a destructive wiper"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/31\/viasat-cyberattack-russian-wiper\/\">targeting the Viasat satellite network<\/a> over Ukraine and Europe.<\/p>\nJohn Hultquist, vice president of intelligence analysis at Mandiant, said in response to the FBI’s operation:<\/p>\nSandworm is the premier Russian cyber attack capability and one of the actors we have been most concerned about in light of the invasion. We are concerned that they could be used to hit targets in Ukraine, but we are also concerned they may hit targets in the West in retribution for the pressure being placed on Russia.<\/p><\/blockquote>\nLast April, the FBI launched"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/04\/13\/fbi-launches-operation-to-remotely-remove-microsoft-exchange-server-backdoors\/\">the first-of-its kind operation<\/a> to copy and remove a backdoor left behind by Chinese spies, who had"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/03\/02\/microsoft-says-china-backed-hackers-are-exploiting-exchange-zero-days\/\">mass-hacked<\/a> thousands of vulnerable Exchange servers in order to steal contact lists and email inboxes.<\/p>\n"border: 1px solid #aaa; width: 100%; height: calc(100vh - 100px); max-height: 700px;\" title=\"DOJ order to remove Cyclops Blink malware (Hosted by DocumentCloud)\" src=\"https:\/\/embed.documentcloud.org\/documents\/21579373-doj-order-to-remove-cyclops-blink-malware\/?embed=1&responsive=1&title=1\" width=\"540\" height=\"700\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts allow-same-origin allow-popups allow-forms allow-popups-to-escape-sandbox\"><\/iframe><\/p>\nUpdated and corrected to clarify that compromised devices were not accessed as part of the FBI’s operation.<\/em><\/p>\nRead more:<\/strong><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/31\/viasat-cyberattack-russian-wiper\/\">Viasat cyberattack blamed on Russian wiper malware<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/04\/13\/fbi-launches-operation-to-remotely-remove-microsoft-exchange-server-backdoors\/\">FBI launches operation to remove backdoors from hacked Microsoft Exchange servers<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2018\/10\/04\/uk-says-russias-gru-was-behind-a-spate-of-chaotic-cyber-attacks-between-2015-and-2017\/\">UK says Russia\u2019s GRU was behind a spate of chaotic cyber attacks between 2015 and 2017<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"The Federal Bureau of Investigation has disclosed it carried out an operation in March to target a massive\u00a0botnet controlled by Russian intelligence. The operation was authorized by courts in California and Pennsylvania, allowing the FBI to copy and remove the so-called Cyclops Blink malware from its command and control servers, also known as C2s, of justice","slug":"department-of-justice","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/865614"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=865614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=865614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=865614"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=865614"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":449557099,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/europe\/","name":"Europe","slug":"europe","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/449557099"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=449557099"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=449557099"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=449557099"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=449557099"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2294145,"date":"2022-04-05T03:00:40","date_gmt":"2022-04-05T10:00:40","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2294145"},"modified":"2022-04-05T11:41:55","modified_gmt":"2022-04-05T18:41:55","slug":"nso-pegasus-jordan-apple","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/04\/05\/nso-pegasus-jordan-apple\/","title":{"rendered":"NSO hacked new Pegasus victims weeks after Apple sought injunction"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">"featured__span-first-words\">Investigators say they<\/span> have found evidence that a Jordanian journalist and human rights defender’s iPhone was hacked with the Pegasus spyware just weeks after Apple sued the spyware’s maker NSO Group to stop it from targeting Apple’s customers.<\/p>\nAward-winning journalist Suhair Jaradat’s phone was hacked with the notorious spyware as recently as December 5, 2021, according to an analysis of her phone by"https:\/\/www.frontlinedefenders.org\/en\/statement-report\/report-jordanian-human-rights-defenders-and-journalists-hacked-pegasus-spyware\">Front Line Defenders and Citizen Lab<\/a> that was shared with TechCrunch ahead of its publication. Jaradat was sent a WhatsApp message from someone impersonating a popular anti-government critic with links to the Pegasus spyware, compromising her phone. According to the forensic analysis, Jaradat’s iPhone was hacked several times in the preceding months and as far back as February 2021.<\/p>\nApple had filed a lawsuit against Israeli spyware maker NSO Group in November 2021, seeking a court-issued injunction aimed at banning NSO from using Apple’s products and services to develop and deploy hacks against its customers.<\/p>\nThe injunction would, if granted, make it more difficult for NSO to deploy its spyware since its stealthiest capabilities rely on abusing Apple’s own services, such as iMessage, to create Apple user accounts for delivering the malware. But so far the case has gotten off to a slow start after the first judge assigned to the case recused herself, with no decision on the case likely to be made any time before June.<\/p>\nThe NSO-built Pegasus spyware gives its government customers"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/19\/toolkit-nso-pegasus-iphone-android\/\">near-complete access<\/a> to a target\u2019s device, including their personal data, photos, messages and precise location. Many victims have received text messages with malicious links, but Pegasus has more recently been able to silently hack iPhones without any user interaction, or so-called “zero-click” attacks.<\/p>\nApple last year bolstered iPhone security by introducing BlastDoor, a new but unseen security feature designed to filter out malicious payloads sent over iMessage that could compromise a device. But NSO was found to have circumvented the security measure with a new exploit, which"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/08\/24\/nso-pegasus-bahrain-iphone-security\/\">researchers named ForcedEntry<\/a> for its ability to break through BlastDoor’s protections. Apple"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/09\/13\/apple-zero-day-nso-pegasus\/\">fixed BlastDoor<\/a> in September after the NSO exploit was found to affect iPads, Macs, and Apple Watches, not just iPhones.<\/p>\nApple declined to comment on the record when reached about the new report prior to publication.<\/p>\nJaradat is one of several Jordanians, including human rights defenders, lawyers and fellow journalists whose phones were compromised likely by agencies of the Jordanian government, according to Front Line Defenders and Citizen Lab’s findings out Tuesday.<\/p>\nAmong the others targeted include Malik Abu Orabi, a human rights lawyer whose work has included defending the teachers’ union, which in 2019 led the longest public sector strike in the country’s history. Abu Orabi’s phone was targeted as early as August 2019 until June 2021. Also, the phone of Ahmed Al-Neimat, a human rights defender and anti-corruption activist, was targeted by the ForcedEntry exploit in February 2021. The researchers said the hacking of Al-Neimat’s phone is believed to be the earliest suspected use of ForcedEntry.<\/p>\nAnother Jordanian journalist and human rights defender’s phone was targeted, according to the researchers, but who asked for her identity not to be disclosed.<\/p>\nApple is the latest tech giant to file suit against NSO for hacking into its customers’ phones. NSO is also currently embroiled in a legal battle with Facebook for using"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/10\/29\/whatsapp-spyware-nso-group\/\">a then-unknown vulnerability in WhatsApp<\/a> to hack into some 1,400 phones belonging to members of civil society. Last year, a U.S. appeals court rejected NSO’s claim that it was entitled to protection of a foreign sovereign, in this case Israel.<\/p>\nNSO has long said it sells its spyware only to law enforcement and intelligence agencies. In a statement, spokesperson Liron Bruck said that while the company has not seen the report, “the use of cyber tools in order to monitor dissidents, activists and journalists is a severe misuse of any technology and goes against the desired use of such critical tools.” NSO declined to answer our specific questions.<\/p>\nA spokesperson for the Jordanian Embassy in Washington DC did not comment by press time, but provided a statement after publication, in which it “categorically denied” the allegations, adding that the kingdom “has not cooperated with any agents with the aim of spying on citizens’ phones or censoring their calls.”<\/p>\nAccording to the U.S. State Department, Jordan is accused of “significant human rights issues,” including censorship, restrictions on free speech, and “substantial restrictions on freedom of association and freedom of peaceful assembly.”<\/p>\nUpdated with comment from the Jordanian government.<\/em><\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"CnLEYLO7Js\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/09\/13\/apple-zero-day-nso-pegasus\/\">Apple patches an NSO zero-day flaw affecting all devices<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Apple patches an NSO zero-day flaw affecting all devices” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/09\/13\/apple-zero-day-nso-pegasus\/embed\/#?secret=RE9AYZr8RE#?secret=CnLEYLO7Js\" data-secret=\"CnLEYLO7Js\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Investigators say they have found evidence that a Jordanian journalist and human rights defender’s iPhone was hacked with the Pegasus spyware just weeks after Apple sued the spyware’s maker NSO Group to stop it from targeting Apple’s customers. Award-winning journalist Suhair Jaradat’s phone was hacked with the notorious spyware as recently as December 5, 2021, lands $10.5M to build out its end-to-end encrypted workspaces"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Six months after"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/26\/skiff-encrypted-collaboration-seed\/\">launching its end-to-end encrypted document editor<\/a>, Skiff has bagged another $10.5 million in fresh funding to build out private and collaborative workspaces for its burgeoning customer base.<\/p>\nWe"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/26\/skiff-encrypted-collaboration-seed\/\">wrote about Skiff last year<\/a> ahead of its launch: Skiff is a web app that has much of the same document-writing and sharing capabilities as Google Docs but is built on a foundation of"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/end-to-end-encryption\/\">end-to-end encryption<\/a>, so Skiff does not have access to users’ documents like Google does. The startup already has more than 20,000 people using its platform, leaps ahead of the 8,000 waitlisted users it had when we first spoke to the company last May.<\/p>\nBut it’s the end-to-end encryption platform that Skiff relies on that holds the keys to the company’s future. Now with $10.5 million in Series A funding in the bank, Skiff’s co-founders Andrew Milich and Jason Ginsberg tell TechCrunch that the company is working toward becoming the “application layer for the decentralized web.”<\/p>\nA core part of the company’s efforts have been on decentralization, a process that allows its users to take ownership of their data. Over the past year Skiff has partnered with Protocol Labs to offer decentralized storage, known as IPFS, or the"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/01\/19\/brave-web-browser-adds-native-support-for-peer-to-peer-ipfs-protocol\/\">Interplanetary File System<\/a>, which allows Skiff to encrypt their documents and scatter them across a network of storage hosts, as well as integrating MetaMask, letting users sign in to Skiff using a portable crypto wallet instead of an email address.<\/p>\n“The way we look at it is Web 2.0 is really about moving information around and web3 is about moving value around,” said Ginsberg, Skiff’s CTO, in a call. “Data is the most valuable thing on the internet, and our goal is that you really should own your own data.”<\/p>\nGinsberg said the company is focused on growing its product offering, such as communication, and allowing users to share more kinds of data on its platform.<\/p>\n“We see hundreds of millions of people choosing privacy products not really meeting the needs of working together remotely, and so that’s really where we see Skiff coming in. There’s tons of different products that we could do along those lines. We’re most interested right now in exploring products that not just deal with the document side of things, but also the communication side,” said Ginsberg.<\/p>\nMilich, the startup’s chief executive, said the round \u2014 led by Sequoia as a returning investor \u2014 will help the company build out those new products that also rely on end-to-end encryption, like communication. Skiff currently has a team of 15 employees dotted across the globe, Milich said. The Series A brings Skiff’s total funding to about $14 million.<\/p>\n\u201cSkiff is building an amazing team and visionary products to lead this moment,” said Konstantine Buhler, a partner at Sequoia. “We couldn\u2019t be more excited to double down.”<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"ovSVa2qy9F\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/26\/skiff-encrypted-collaboration-seed\/\">Skiff, an end-to-end encrypted alternative to Google Docs, raises $3.7M seed<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Skiff, an end-to-end encrypted alternative to Google Docs, raises $3.7M seed” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/26\/skiff-encrypted-collaboration-seed\/embed\/#?secret=NwsyJlSLJN#?secret=ovSVa2qy9F\" data-secret=\"ovSVa2qy9F\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Six months after launching its end-to-end encrypted document editor, Skiff has bagged another $10.5 million in fresh funding to build out private and collaborative workspaces for its burgeoning customer base. We wrote about Skiff last year ahead of its launch: Skiff is a web app that has much of the same document-writing and sharing capabilities capital","slug":"sequoia-capital","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/245607"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=245607"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=245607"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=245607"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=245607"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2290002,"date":"2022-03-28T12:00:11","date_gmt":"2022-03-28T19:00:11","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2290002"},"modified":"2022-03-29T15:22:09","modified_gmt":"2022-03-29T22:22:09","slug":"lapsus-passwords-okta-breach","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/28\/lapsus-passwords-okta-breach\/","title":{"rendered":"Lapsus$ found a spreadsheet of accounts as they breached Okta, documents show"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">The Lapsus$ hackers used compromised credentials to break into the network of customer service giant Sitel in January, days before subsequently accessing the internal systems of authentication giant Okta, according to documents seen by TechCrunch that provide new details of the cyber intrusion that have not yet been reported.<\/p>\nCustomers only learned of Okta’s January security breach on March 22 after the Lapsus$ hacking group"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/22\/okta-january-hack-breach\/\">published screenshots<\/a> revealing it had accessed Okta’s internal apps and systems some two months earlier. Okta admitted the compromise in"https:\/\/support.okta.com\/help\/s\/article\/Frequently-Asked-Questions-Regarding-January-2022-Compromise?language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a blog post<\/a>, and later confirmed 366 of its corporate customers are affected by the breach, or about 2.5% of its customer base.<\/p>\nThe documents provide the most detailed account to date of the Sitel compromise, which allowed the hackers to later gain access to Okta’s network.<\/p>\nOkta is used by thousands of organizations and governments worldwide as a single sign-on provider, allowing employees to securely access a company\u2019s internal systems, such as email accounts, applications, databases and more.<\/p>\nThe documents, obtained by independent security researcher"https:\/\/twitter.com\/BillDemirkapi\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Bill Demirkapi<\/a> and shared with TechCrunch, include a Sitel customer communication sent on January 25 — more than a week after hackers first compromised its network — and a detailed timeline of the Sitel intrusion compiled by incident response firm Mandiant dated March 17 that was shared with Okta.<\/p>\nAccording to the documents, Sitel said it discovered the security incident in its VPN gateways on a legacy network belonging to Sykes, a customer service company working for Okta that Sitel acquired in 2021. VPNs, or virtual private networks, are often"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/07\/23\/corporate-vpn-flaws-risk\/\">a target for attackers<\/a> since they can be exploited to remotely access a company’s network.<\/p>\nThe timeline details how the attackers used remote access services and publicly accessible hacking tools to compromise and navigate through Sitel’s network, gaining deeper visibility to the network over the five days that Lapsus$ had access. Sitel said that its Azure cloud infrastructure was also compromised by hackers.<\/p>\nAccording to the timeline, the hackers accessed a spreadsheet on Sitel’s internal network early on January 21 called “DomAdmins-LastPass.xlsx.” The filename suggests that the spreadsheet contained passwords for domain administrator accounts that were exported from a Sitel employee’s LastPass password manager. Sitel spokesperson Matt Jaffe did not dispute this characterization when reached by TechCrunch prior to publication, but instead declined to comment. A day after publication, Sitel said in a statement that the spreadsheet “simply listed account names from legacy Sykes but did not contain any passwords,” but did not offer any evidence for this claim.<\/p>\nAbout five hours later, the hackers created a new Sykes user account and added the account to a user group called “tenant administrators,” which have broad access to the organization, likely to create a “backdoor” account to Sitel’s network that the hackers could use if they were later discovered and locked out. The Lapsus$ hackers were compromising Okta’s network at around the same time, according to"https:\/\/support.okta.com\/help\/s\/article\/Frequently-Asked-Questions-Regarding-January-2022-Compromise?language=en_US\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Okta’s timeline<\/a> of events.<\/p>\nThe timeline shows that the hackers last accessed Sitel’s network on January 21 at 2 p.m. (UTC), around 14 hours after accessing the spreadsheet. Sitel issued a company-wide password reset to try to lock out the attackers.<\/p>\nOkta has faced criticism for not warning customers sooner of the Sitel breach following its receipt of Mandiant’s report dated March 17. Okta chief security officer David Bradbury said the company “should have moved more swiftly to understand its implications.”<\/p>\nOkta was unable to comment when reached prior to publication. Mandiant also did not dispute the contents of the reports but declined to comment.<\/p>\nOkta is just one of several big-name companies targeted by the Lapsus$ hacking and extortion group in recent months. The Lapsus$ group first emerged on the hacking scene in December after targeting Brazil\u2019s Ministry of Health in a cyberattack that stole 50 terabytes of data, including citizens\u2019 vaccination information. Since then, the gang has targeted"https:\/\/www.wired.com\/story\/lapsus-hacking-group-extortion-nvidia-samsung\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">several Portuguese-language companies,<\/a> as well as Big Tech giants including"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/07\/samsung-breach-source-code\/\">Samsung<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/04\/nvidia-ransomware-hackers-demands\/\">Nvidia<\/a>,"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/23\/microsoft-lapsus-hack-source-code\/\">Microsoft<\/a> and"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/23\/okta-breach-sykes-sitel\/\">Okta<\/a>, touting its access and stolen data to the tens of thousands of subscribers of its Telegram channel, while often making unusual demands in exchange for not publishing their victims’ stolen files,<\/p>\nU.K. police said last week they had"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/24\/london-police-lapsus-arrests\/\">arrested seven people<\/a> connected to the incidents, all aged between 16 and 21.<\/p>\nUpdated headline with comment from Sitel’s comment, sent a day after publication.<\/em><\/p>\n\/>\nIf you know more about the breach or work at Okta or Sitel, get in touch with the security desk on Signal at +1 646-755-8849 or zack.whittaker@techcrunch.com by email.<\/i><\/i><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"The Lapsus$ hackers used compromised credentials to break into the network of customer service giant Sitel in January, days before subsequently accessing the internal systems of authentication giant Okta, according to documents seen by TechCrunch that provide new details of the cyber intrusion that have not yet been reported. Customers only learned of Okta’s January -media-gallery?tags=42674"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=42674"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2288663,"date":"2022-03-23T13:36:21","date_gmt":"2022-03-23T20:36:21","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2288663"},"modified":"2022-03-23T14:04:53","modified_gmt":"2022-03-23T21:04:53","slug":"okta-breach-sykes-sitel","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/23\/okta-breach-sykes-sitel\/","title":{"rendered":"Okta says hundreds of companies impacted by security breach"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Okta says 366 corporate customers, or about 2.5% of its customer base, were impacted by a security breach that allowed hackers to access the company’s internal network.<\/p>\nThe authentication giant"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/22\/okta-january-hack-breach\/\">admitted the compromise<\/a> after the Lapsus$ hacking and extortion group posted screenshots of Okta’s apps and systems on Monday, some two months after the hackers first gained access to its network.<\/p>\nThe breach was initially blamed on an unnamed subprocessor that provides customer support services to Okta. In an"https:\/\/www.okta.com\/blog\/2022\/03\/oktas-investigation-of-the-january-2022-compromise\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">updated statement<\/a> on Wednesday, Okta’s chief security officer David Bradbury confirmed the subprocessor is a company called Sykes, which last year was acquired by Miami-based contact center giant Sitel.<\/p>\nCustomer support companies like Sykes and Sitel often have wide access to the organizations that they support for facilitating customer requests. Malicious hackers have previously targeted customer support companies, which often have weaker cybersecurity defenses than some of the highly-secured companies that they support.
"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2019\/04\/13\/microsoft-support-agent-email-hack\/\">Microsoft<\/a> and"https:\/\/www.vice.com\/en\/article\/qj4ddw\/hacker-bribed-roblox-insider-accessed-user-data-reset-passwords\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Roblox<\/a> have both experienced similar targeted compromises of customer support agents’ accounts that led to access of their internal systems.<\/p>\nIn Okta’s case, the"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/23\/microsoft-lapsus-hack-source-code\/\">Lapsus$ hackers<\/a> were in Sitel’s network for five days over January 16-21, 2022 until the hackers were detected and booted from its network, according to Bradbury.<\/p>\nOkta faced considerable criticism from the wider security industry for its handling of the compromise and the months-long delay in notifying customers, which found out at the same time when"https:\/\/blog.cloudflare.com\/cloudflare-investigation-of-the-january-2022-okta-compromise\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">news broke on social media<\/a>. According to Bradbury, Sitel engaged an unnamed forensics firm to investigate, which concluded on March 10. Only a week later was the report turned over to Okta on March 17.<\/p>\nBradbury said he is “greatly disappointed by the long period of time that transpired between our notification to Sitel and the issuance of the complete investigation report,” and admitted that Okta “should have moved more swiftly” to understand the report’s implications.<\/p>\nBut an email from a Sitel representative disputed how Okta characterized the report, saying that the security breach “did not impact legacy Sitel Group systems or networks; only legacy Sykes’ network was affected.” (The Sitel representative declared their email “off the record,” which requires both parties to agree to the terms in advance. We are printing the responses since we were given no opportunity to decline.) The email added: “We have not found evidence of a security breach of client’s systems or networks on legacy Sykes or Sitel Group side.” The email also said that the Sitel has no evidence of a data breach, but the company declined to say if it has the means, such as logs, to determine what, if any, data was accessed or exfiltrated by the attackers. Sitel would not name the forensics firm that investigated the breach.<\/p>\nAn earlier statement attributed to Sitel spokesperson Rebecca Sanders said: “As a result of the investigation, along with our ongoing assessment of external threats, we are confident there is no longer a security risk. We are unable to comment on our relationship with any specific brands or the nature of the services we provide for our clients.”<\/p>\nOkta has not yet responded to TechCrunch’s questions regarding the breach.<\/p>\n"embed breakout\">\n"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"vWGiLUPBwP\">"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/22\/okta-january-hack-breach\/\">Okta confirms January breach after hackers publish screenshots of its internal network<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“Okta confirms January breach after hackers publish screenshots of its internal network” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/22\/okta-january-hack-breach\/embed\/#?secret=BFOrJ6ls3I#?secret=vWGiLUPBwP\" data-secret=\"vWGiLUPBwP\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Okta says 366 corporate customers, or about 2.5% of its customer base, were impacted by a security breach that allowed hackers to access the company’s internal network. The authentication giant admitted the compromise after the Lapsus$ hacking and extortion group posted screenshots of Okta’s apps and systems on Monday, some two months after the hackers confirms January breach after hackers publish screenshots of its internal network"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Identity giant Okta has confirmed a January security incident after hackers posted screenshots overnight apparently showing access to the company’s internal systems.<\/p>\nThe Lapsus$ hacking group published several screenshots to its Telegram channel purporting to show internal Okta applications, Jira bug ticketing system, and the company’s Slack on January 21. Lapsus$ claimed it did not steal data from Okta, and that its focus was on targeting Okta customers.<\/p>\nOkta is used by thousands of organizations and governments worldwide as a single sign-on provider, allowing employees to securely access a company’s internal systems, such as email accounts, calendars, applications and more.<\/p>\nOkta chief executive Todd McKinnon"https:\/\/twitter.com\/toddmckinnon\/status\/1506184721922859010\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">confirmed<\/a> the breach in a tweet thread overnight on March 22: “In late January 2022, Okta detected an attempt to compromise the account of a third party customer support engineer working for one of our subprocessors. The matter was investigated and contained by the subprocessor.”<\/p>\n“We believe the screenshots shared online are connected to this January event. Based on our investigation to date, there is no evidence of ongoing malicious activity beyond the activity detected in January.”<\/p>\nOkta has not yet named the subprocessor, and has not yet responded to TechCrunch’s questions about the breach.<\/p>\nIn an updated statement, Okta’s chief security officer David Bradbury said the compromise was with one of Okta’s third-party providers over a five-day window between January 16-21, 2022. Forbes"https:\/\/www.forbes.com\/sites\/thomasbrewster\/2022\/03\/22\/fury-as-okta-the-company-that-manages-100-million-logins-fails-to-tell-customers-about-breach-for-months\/\">is reporting<\/a> that the company in question is Sykes, a company acquired by Sitel Group in July 2021. In a brief statement, Sitel said it was “confident there is no longer a security risk,” but declined to comment on its relationship with its customers, and did not immediately answer our questions.<\/p>\nSecurity researcher"https:\/\/twitter.com\/billdemirkapi\/status\/1506107157124722690?s=27\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Bill Demirkapi<\/a> said that the screenshots contain several artifacts that suggest the hackers may have used Sykes’ remote access tools and VPN to gain access to Okta’s network.<\/p>\nLapsus$ has targeted several big-name companies in recent weeks, including"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/04\/nvidia-ransomware-hackers-demands\/\">Nvidia<\/a> and"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/07\/samsung-breach-source-code\/\">Samsung<\/a>. Just this week Microsoft said"https:\/\/www.vice.com\/en\/article\/y3vk9x\/microsoft-hacked-lapsus-extortion-investigating\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">it was investigating<\/a> a possible security breach.
"https:\/\/www.wired.com\/story\/lapsus-hacking-group-extortion-nvidia-samsung\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">According to Wired<\/a>, the group focused on Portuguese-language targets, including Portuguese media giant Impresa, and the South American telecom companies Claro and Embratel.<\/p>\n\/>\nIf you know more about the Okta breach or work at the company, get in touch with the security desk on Signal at +1 646-755-8849 or zack.whittaker@techcrunch.com by email.<\/em><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Identity giant Okta has confirmed a January security incident after hackers posted screenshots overnight apparently showing access to the company’s internal systems. The Lapsus$ hacking group published several screenshots to its Telegram channel purporting to show internal Okta applications, Jira bug ticketing system, and the company’s Slack on January 21. mckinnon","slug":"todd-mckinnon","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/48408738"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=48408738"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=48408738"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=48408738"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=48408738"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":13847,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/vpn\/","name":"vpn","slug":"vpn","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/13847"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=13847"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=13847"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=13847"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=13847"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2286927,"date":"2022-03-18T20:00:12","date_gmt":"2022-03-19T03:00:12","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2286927"},"modified":"2022-03-21T06:04:57","modified_gmt":"2022-03-21T13:04:57","slug":"india-cisf-security-data-exposed","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/18\/india-cisf-security-data-exposed\/","title":{"rendered":"A security lapse exposed India’s CISF personnel files and health records"},"content":{"rendered":""speakable-summary\">Internal documents, officer health records, and personnel files belonging to India’s Central Industrial Security Force spilled online because of a data security lapse.<\/p>\nA security researcher in India, who asked not to be named for fear of retaliation from the Indian government, found a database packed with network logs generated by a security appliance connected to CISF’s network. But the database was not secured with a password, allowing anyone on the internet to access the logs from their web browser.<\/p>\nThe network logs contain detailed records of which files on CISF’s network were accessed or blocked because of security rules. Because the logs contained full web addresses of documents stored on CISF’s network, it was possible for anyone on the internet to access the logs and then open those files in their browser directly from CISF’s network, also without needing a password.<\/p>\nThe logs contained records for more than 246,000 web addresses of PDF documents on CISF’s network, many of which relate to personnel files and health records and contain personally identifiable information on CISF officers. Some of the files are dated as recently as 2022.<\/p>\nCISF is one of the largest police forces in the world with more than 160,000 personnel, tasked with protecting government facilities, infrastructure and airport security across the country.<\/p>\nThe researcher said the security appliance is built by Haltdos, an India-based security company that provides network security technology to organizations. The database was first found to be exposed on March 6, according to Shodan, a search engine for exposed devices and databases. TechCrunch confirmed that the database was configured with the name “haltdos.”<\/p>\nHaltdos CEO Anshul Saxena did not respond to multiple requests for comment. TechCrunch also emailed a CISF public affairs officer with several web addresses of publicly exposed files stored on its servers, but we did not receive a response. It’s not uncommon for government organizations in India to quietly fix security issues when alerted by good-faith security researchers but then rebuff or deny the claims when they invariably become public knowledge.<\/p>\nThe database and the security appliance are no longer online. In an email sent to TechCrunch after publication, Haltdos described the lapse as a “serious security incident.”<\/p>\nEditor’s note:<\/strong> Updated with remarks from Haltdos.<\/em><\/p>\nRead more:<\/b><\/p>\n\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/22\/stalkerware-network-spilling-data\/\">Behind the stalkerware network spilling the private phone data of hundreds of thousands<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/11\/24\/india-bans-another-43-chinese-apps\/\">India bans 43 more Chinese apps over cybersecurity concerns<\/a><\/li>\n"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/08\/uk-foreign-office-cyber-incident\/\">UK Foreign Office calls in \u2018urgent support\u2019 after cyber incident<\/a><\/li>\nTechCrunch+:"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/11\/why-are-internet-asset-startups-so-hot-right-now\/\">Why are cybersecurity asset management startups so hot right now?<\/a><\/li>\n<\/ul>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"Internal documents, officer health records, and personnel files belonging to India’s Central Industrial Security Force spilled online because of a data security lapse.
